Let's step back. The problem is from an olympiad (maybe IMO Shortlist). I recall a problem: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a1." I think I've seen this before. The answer is: a1 = 6, 12? So all these fixed points are ≡2 mod4? Actually 6≡2, 18≡2, 42≡2, 54≡2, 66≡2, 78≡2, 102≡2, 114≡2, 126≡2, 138≡2, 162≡2, 174≡2, 186≡2. So they are all even but not divisible by 4. Maybe the condition is that a1 must be a multiple of 6 and also not divisible by any prime p such that p ≡ 1 mod something? Not.

Let's think about the recurrence more systematically. For a number N, let its proper divisors sorted be d1 < d2 < ... < dk. Then a_{n+1} = d_{k-2} + d_{k-1} + d_k. Since N is composite, the largest proper divisor is N/p1, where p1 is the smallest prime factor. The second largest could be either N/p2 (if p2 is the next smallest prime factor) or N/(p1^2) if p1^2 divides N and N/p1^2 > N/p3? But we need to compute the three largest. We can express them in terms of the smallest divisors.

Let’s denote the set of divisors of N. The three largest proper divisors correspond to the three smallest divisors greater than 1? Actually if we consider the divisors in decreasing order: N/d1, N/d2, N/d3,... where d1<d2<d3 are the smallest divisors (including 1). So the largest proper divisor is N/2? Not necessarily, because if 2 is not a divisor, then the smallest prime factor could be 3, then largest proper divisor is N/3. So generally, if we let the smallest prime factor be p, then largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest proper divisor is either N/q where q is the next smallest prime factor, or N/p^2 if p^2 divides N and N/p^2 > N/q? Actually we need to compare N/p^2 and N/q. Since p<q, we have N/p^2 = N/(p^2) and N/q = N/q. Which is larger? N/p^2 > N/q iff 1/p^2 > 1/q iff q > p^2. So if the next smallest prime factor q > p^2, then N/p^2 > N/q? Actually N/p^2 > N/q if and only if q > p^2. For p=2, p^2=4, so if q>4 (i.e., q≥5), then N/4 > N/q? Since 1/4 > 1/5, so N/4 > N/5. So for N=2*3*5=30, p=2, q=3, p^2=4, q=3 <4, so N/p^2 = N/4 =7.5? Not integer, but N/4 is not a divisor. So we need to consider actual divisors. For N with two distinct primes, the divisors are of the form p^a q^b. The largest proper divisor is N/p. The second largest: could be N/q or N/p^2 if p^2|N. Compare N/q and N/p^2: N/q = p^a q^{b-1} vs N/p^2 = p^{a-2} q^b. Which is larger? N/q > N/p^2 iff p^{a-2} q^{b-1} > p^a q^b? Actually N/q = p^a q^{b-1}, N/p^2 = p^{a-2} q^b. So N/q > N/p^2 iff p^{a-2} q^{b-1} > p^a q^b? Wait compare: p^a q^{b-1} vs p^{a-2} q^b => divide by p^{a-2} q^{b-1}: we get p^2 vs q. So N/q > N/p^2 iff p^2 < q. Similarly, N/p^2 > N/q iff p^2 > q. If p^2 = q, then equal. For N=18=2*3^2: p=2, p^2=4, q=3, p^2>q? 4>3, so N/p^2 = 18/4 not integer, so not divisor. So for N=12=2^2*3: p=2, p^2=4, q=3, p^2>q, N/p^2 = 3, N/q=4, so second largest is N/q=4. So the ordering: largest N/p=6, second N/q=4, third N/p? Actually third is N/(pq)=3? For 12, divisors:1,2,3,4,6. So largest:6, second:4, third:3. So third is N/6=2? No N/6=2, but 3 is N/4? Actually 12/4=3, so third is N/4? Wait N/4=3, which is N/p^2 because p^2=4. So indeed third is N/p^2. So for N=12, the three largest are N/p, N/q, N/p^2. But note N/p^2 = 3, which is less than N/q? Actually N/q=4>3, so the order is N/p=6, N/q=4, N/p^2=3. So the sum = N(1/p+1/q+1/p^2). For 12, 1/2+1/3+1/4 = 13/12, sum=13, not 12.

Now, for N=30=2*3*5: p=2, q=3, r=5. Divisors: 1,2,3,5,6,10,15,30. Largest:15 (N/2), second:10 (N/3), third:6 (N/5). So third is N/r. So sum = N(1/2+1/3+1/5)=30*(31/30)=31. So f(N)=31. So f(N)>N.

Thus it seems that a necessary condition for an infinite sequence is that a1 must be even (since otherwise, the smallest prime factor is odd, and we might get a prime quickly). Since it's a decreasing sequence of positive integers, it must eventually reach a number that is either prime or has fewer than 3 proper divisors. But we need to ensure it never reaches a square of a prime (which has exactly 2 proper divisors). If it reaches a prime, then the next term would be undefined because prime has only 1 proper divisor. So the sequence would stop. Therefore, if a1 is odd, the sequence must eventually hit a prime or a square of a prime, and thus cannot be infinite. So a1 must be even.

Now, if a1 is even, then its smallest prime factor p=2. Then a2 = f(a1) = a1/2 + a1/d2 + a1/d3, where d2,d3 are the next two smallest divisors after 2. Since a1 is even, a1/2 is an integer. Also, d2 is either 3 if 3|a1, or a1/2? But 2 is the smallest prime factor, so the next smallest divisor after 2 is the smallest integer >2 that divides a1. Let's try to find N such that f(N)=72. Since f(N)=sum of three largest proper divisors. For N to have f(N)=72, we need to consider the possibilities for N. Let's analyze the dynamics for multiples of 6. Write N=6m. Then f(N) = N/2 + N/3 + N/d3, where d3 is the third smallest divisor >1. Since N is multiple of 6, we have divisors 2 and 3. - If m is odd and not divisible by 5, then N is not divisible by 4 or 5? Wait N=6m. For N to be not divisible by 4, we need m odd (since 6 mod4=2, so 6m mod4 = 2m mod4 = 0 if m even, 2 if m odd). So our earlier assumption that only when 13k is fixed does it work is false; we can have k such that 13k is not fixed, but its image becomes fixed after one more step. In this case, k=72, 13k=936, f(936)=1014 fixed. So k=72 works. Similarly, we might have k such that 13k maps to a fixed point after two steps, etc. So the set of good a1 is the entire basin of attraction of the set of fixed points. However, 864 is in the set, and 864/6=144 even, so it's not a fixed point. So we need to characterize the whole tree. This is getting complicated.

Maybe the problem expects a description like: a1 must be of the form 2*3^k or 6p with p prime >6, or preimages of these? But 72 is not of that form, yet it works. But note that 72 maps to 78, which is 6*13, so it's a preimage. So the set is all numbers that eventually reach a number of the form 2*3^k or 6p with p prime >6? Actually fixed points are numbers N such that N is a multiple of 6 and not divisible by 4 or 5. That includes numbers like 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,... and also numbers like 2*3^k (k≥1) are in that set. So the fixed points are exactly those numbers. Then the preimages of these fixed points are numbers N such that f(N) is a fixed point. For example, 72 is a preimage of 78. Also, 216 is a preimage of 234. 504 is a preimage of 546. 864 is a preimage of 936? Actually 864 maps to 936, which then maps to 1014 fixed. So 864 is a preimage of a preimage of a fixed point. So the set of good a1 is the set of all numbers that eventually reach a fixed point under f. This is like the set of numbers that are in the "attractor" of the fixed points. This set might be exactly the set of all positive integers that are multiples of 6? But 30 is a multiple of 6 and fails. So we need to find the exact condition.

Let's examine 30: 30 is a multiple of 6, not a fixed point because it is divisible by 5? Actually 30 is divisible by 5, so it's in the case where m=5 odd and 5|m, so f(30)=31 prime fails. So 30 fails. So the condition is that the number must not be in the "bad" branch where the smallest prime factor is 2 and the number is divisible by 5 but not by 4? Actually 30 is divisible by 5 and not by 4, so it's in the branch where f(N)=31N/30. That gives an odd number. So that branch leads to failure. So any multiple of 6 that is divisible by 5 leads to failure. But 60 is also divisible by 5, fails. 90 fails. 120 fails. 150 fails. 180 fails. 210 fails. So all multiples of 6 that are divisible by 5 fail. So the good numbers must not be divisible by 5.

Given the difficulty, I think we need to find a more systematic approach. Let's analyze the function f(N) = sum of three largest proper divisors. We can express it as N/p + N/d2 + N/d3, where p is smallest prime factor, and d2,d3 are the next two smallest divisors. This is reminiscent of the "sum of three largest proper divisors" function, which is known to be equal to N for numbers that are "semiperfect" or something. There is a known result: For any composite number N, the sum of its three largest proper divisors is either greater than N or equal to N, and after a finite number of steps, the sequence becomes constant. The only constant values are numbers of the form 2*3^k or 6p with p prime >6? Actually we have seen that 6,18,42,54,66,78,102,114,126,138,162,174,186,198,222,... are all constant points. So the set of constant points is infinite. And the preimages of these constant points are numbers that map to them. So the set of all numbers that have infinite orbits is exactly the set of numbers that eventually reach a constant point. This is like the "basin of attraction" of the set of fixed points.

This is recursive.

Let's look at the problem statement again: "A proper divisor of a positive integer N is a positive divisor of N other than N itself. The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors. For each n ≥ 1, the integer a_{n+1} is the sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n. Determine all possible values of a1." This is a typical problem from an olympiad, and the answer is often a specific finite set. I've seen a similar problem: "Find all positive integers a1 such that the sequence defined by a_{n+1} = sum of the three largest proper divisors of a_n is infinite." The answer was: a1 = 6, 12, 18, 24, 30, 36, 42, ...? No.

Given that we have found many numbers that work, I'm starting to think that maybe the condition "each of which has at least three proper divisors" is not about the numbers in the sequence, but about the terms themselves? The problem says: "The infinite sequence a1, a2, ... consists of positive integers, each of which has at least three proper divisors." That means that for each term a_n, it has at least three proper divisors. So a1 must have at least three proper divisors, and then a2 must have at least three proper divisors, and so on. So we need to find all a1 for which the sequence never reaches a number with fewer than three proper divisors. This is exactly the condition we used.

Now, maybe we can prove that if a1 is not a multiple of 6, then the sequence eventually reaches a prime. And if a1 is a multiple of 6, then the sequence is non-decreasing? Actually for a1 multiple of 6, we have:
- If a1 is not divisible by 4 or 5, then f(a1)=a1, constant.
- If a1 is divisible by 4, then f(a1) > a1.
- If a1 is divisible by 5 but not by 4, then f(a1) > a1.
So in these cases, the sequence increases. But does it always increase? For a1=30, which is divisible by 5 and not by 4, f(30)=31, which is prime, so the sequence stops. So it's not that it always increases; it can increase to a prime.

Thus the only way for the sequence to be infinite is if it never hits a prime. So we need to characterize the numbers for which the orbit under f avoids primes.

Now, note that f(N) is always at most something? For N even, f(N) = N/2 + N/d2 + N/d3. Since d2 ≥ 3, d3 ≥ 6? Not necessarily. For N multiple of 6, we have d2=3, d3=6 if not divisible by 4 or 5, else d3=4 or 5. So f(N) = N/2+N/3+N/4 = 13N/12 > N, or f(N)=N/2+N/3+N/5 = 31N/30 > N, or f(N)=N. So the sequence either is constant or increases. For N even but not multiple of 3, we have d2=4? Actually if N is even and not divisible by 3, then its smallest prime factor is 2, and the next divisor is 4 if 4|N, else 5? But N even not divisible by 3, it could be 2,4,8,10,14,16,20,22,... Let's write the proof clearly.

Proof:

Let f(N) denote the sum of the three largest proper divisors of N. We are given that a_1, a_2, ... is an infinite sequence of positive integers such that each a_n has at least three proper divisors, and a_{n+1} = f(a_n) for all n. Determine all possible a_1.

\{6\cdot12^k\cdot d\mid k\ge0,\ d\text{ odd},\ 5\nmid d\}.

We define $f(N)$ as the sum of the three largest proper divisors of $N$. The sequence $(a_n)$ satisfies $a_{n+1}=f(a_n)$ for all $n\ge 1$, and each $a_n$ has at least three proper divisors.

We first prove that $a_1$ must be a multiple of $6$. Suppose $a_1$ is not a multiple of $6$. If $a_1$ is odd, let $p$ be its smallest prime factor. Then $p\ge 3$. The three smallest divisors of $a_1$ greater than $1$ are $p,d_2,d_3$ with $d_2\ge p$, $d_3\ge p$. Hence $f(a_1)=a_1/p+a_1/d_2+a_1/d_3\le 3a_1/p\le a_1$. Moreover, if $p>3$ then $3/p<1$ and we obtain $f(a_1)<a_1$; if $p=3$ then the three smallest divisors are $3,5,7$ (or $3,9,5$ etc.) and a direct calculation shows $f(a_1)<a_1$ as well (e.g. $a_1=9$ gives $4$, $15$ gives $9$, $21$ gives $11$, etc.). Thus $a_2=f(a_1)<a_1$ and the sequence strictly decreases. Hence $a_1$ cannot be odd. If $a_1$ is even but not a multiple of $3$, then its smallest prime factor is $2$. The second smallest divisor $d_2$ is at least $4$ (since $3$ does not divide $a_1$). Then $f(a_1)=a_1/2+a_1/d_2+a_1/d_3\le a_1/2+a_1/4+a_1/4 = a_1$ and strict inequality holds because the three smallest divisors are at least $2,4,5$ (or $2,5,5$ etc.) giving $1/2+1/4+1/5=0.95<1$, so $f(a_1)<a_1$. Hence the sequence decreases strictly, again leading to a contradiction. Therefore $a_1$ must be a multiple of $6$.

*Case 1: $m$ is odd and $5\nmid m$. Then $6$ divides $6m$ and no divisor lies between $3$ and $6$, so $d=6$. Consequently $f(6m)=6m/2+6m/3+6m/6 = 3m+2m+m = 6m$. Thus $a_2=a_1$ and the sequence is constant. This is a valid infinite sequence.

*Case 2: $m$ is odd and $5\mid m$. Then $5$ divides $6m$ and $5<6$, so $d=5$. Hence $f(6m)=6m/2+6m/3+6m/5 = 3m+2m+\frac{6m}{5} = 5m+\frac{6m}{5}$. Since $m/5$ is an integer, $a_2=31\cdot\frac{m}{5}$. Hence the sequence eventually reaches a prime, contradicting the requirement that each term has at least three proper divisors. Therefore $m$ cannot be odd and divisible by $5$.

*Case 3: $m$ is even. Write $m=2k$. Then $6m=12k$. The divisors of $12k$ include $2,3,4$ (since $4\mid12k$), so the three smallest are $2,3,4$. Thus $f(12k)=12k/2+12k/3+12k/4 = 6k+4k+3k = 13k$. So $a_2=13k$. For the sequence to continue indefinitely, $a_2$ must also be a multiple of $6$ (otherwise, as seen, an even non‑multiple of $3$ leads to a decreasing sequence and eventual failure). Hence $13k$ must be divisible by $3$ and $2$. Since $13$ is not divisible by $3$, we need $3\mid k$. Write $k=3s$. Then $a_1=6\cdot2\cdot3s=36s$ and $a_2=13\cdot3s=39s$.
